This resulted in a total of 31,237 gene models of which 19,768 have either EST or protein support (5,316 gene models have castor bean EST support determined by PASA, and 16,848 have protein evidence support determined by AAT searches). 3,150 models were labeled as 'partial' because they missed either start and/or stop codons. 354 gene models contained an internal gap, which is represented by 'Ns' in the nucleotide sequence and 'Xs' in protein sequence, indicating the location and predicted size of the gap.</p><p>A dataset of 60 castor bean genes manually modeled based on highly conserved cDNA and protein alignments across multiple plant species were used as reference to evaluate the gene prediction algorithms' performance in comparison with EVM consensus predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.1674#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Although this is a small set of genes, we used the exons to estimate the specificity and sensitivity of exon prediction by the different gene-finder programs as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2095">65</a></sup>. Future iterations of the annotation can be improved by using a larger set of genes for training and evaluation of the gene prediction software, as more castor bean cDNA sequences become available.</p><p>Gene models were automatically named and their function was assigned by computationally extracting this information from BLASTP searches against the TAIR6 <i>Arabidopsis</i> peptides, Uniprot-Swissprot and experimentally verified Panda (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/panda">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/panda</a>), Panther (<a href="http://www.pantherdb.org/">http://www.pantherdb.org/</a>) and Interpro (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro</a>) databases. Gene models whose hits in those databases were defined as “unknown function” were labeled “conserved hypothetical protein” in our genome annotation. Gene models with no match in these databases above the selected threshold were labeled “hypothetical protein.”</p><p>Automated Gene Ontology GO term assignments were done by extrapolating GO terms from matching <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins using BLASTP with an E value threshold of 10<sup>−40</sup>. Castor bean gene models with no match to <i>Arabidopsis</i> were screened against Pfam domains and assigned the Pfam associated GO term, if matches were above the selected cutoff. Altogether, this resulted in the assignment of 43,657 GO terms to 14,991 <i>R. communis</i> proteins.</p><p>Putative signal peptide sequences were identified using SignalP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Bendtsen, J.D., Nielsen, H., von Heijne, G. &amp; Brunak, S. Improved prediction of signal peptides: SignalP 3.0. J. Mol. Biol. 340, 783–795 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2144">66</a></sup> and TargetP (<a href="http://www.cbs.dtu.dk/services/TargetP">http://www.cbs.dtu.dk/services/TargetP</a>), and transmembrane regions were predicted by tmHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Krogh, A., Larsson, B., von Heijne, G. &amp; Sonnhammer, E.L. Predicting transmembrane protein topology with a hidden Markov model: application to complete genomes. J. Mol. Biol. 305, 567–580 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2155">67</a></sup>. Castor bean protein domains were also compared against the Pfam database of conserved families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Finn, R.D. et al. Pfam: clans, web tools and services. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D247–D251 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2159">68</a></sup>. Proteins were organized into putative paralogous families based on conserved domain composition, taking into account both previously identified domains from public databases and potential novel domains identified using independent methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Complete reannotation of the Arabidopsis genome: methods, tools, protocols and the final release. BMC Biol. 3, 7 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2163">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wortman, J.R. et al. Annotation of the Arabidopsis genome. Plant Physiol. 132, 461–468 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2166">70</a></sup>.</p><p>Noncoding RNAs were identified by searching against various RNA libraries. tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2173">71</a></sup> was run on the assembled genomic sequence to identify tRNAs. All 20 tRNAs were found in the genome with a total of 717 copies. rRNA sequences were annotated based on homology to previously published rRNA sequences in plants. snRNA were searched by blasting against the NONCODE database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="He, S. et al. NONCODE v2.0: decoding the non-coding. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, Database issue, D170–D172 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2177">72</a></sup>.</p><p>We assigned Enzyme Commission (EC) classification developed by the Nomenclature Committee of the International Union of Biochemistry and Molecular Biology, to provide metabolic pathway annotation. Castor bean proteins were searched against PRIAM profiles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Claudel-Renard, C., Chevalet, C., Faraut, T. &amp; Kahn, D. Enzyme-specific profiles for genome annotation: PRIAM. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 6633–6639 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2184">73</a></sup> using PSI-BLAST, and EC numbers were assigned for hits with an E value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>.</p><p>Annotation data are displayed in the project website (<a href="http://castorbean.jcvi.org/">http://castorbean.jcvi.org/</a>), which includes a generic genome browser (<a href="http://gmod.org/wiki/GBrowse">http://gmod.org/wiki/GBrowse</a>), where gene models can be viewed in their sequence and genomic context. We used a gene model nomenclature that is composed by the scaffold ID number, followed by a period and the gene model number that consists of the letter 'm' followed by the gene model number. This number can be used to locate genes in the castor bean genome browser. Gene models in the genome browser are linked to Manatee pages, which include additional annotation information (<a href="http://manatee.sourceforge.net">http://manatee.sourceforge.net</a>).</p><p>The castor bean predicted proteome could be matched to over 3,000 protein domains from Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Finn, R.D. et al. Pfam: clans, web tools and services. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D247–D251 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2217">68</a></sup>, several of which are not present in <i>Arabidopsis</i> or poplar, including secondary metabolism genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.1674#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). However, these results may have a substantial error due to inaccuracies of the automatic annotation both in poplar and castor bean.</p><p>We also searched for tandem gene duplications and found a total of 2,610 (8% of the total) genes forming part of tandem arrays.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Identification of genome duplications.</h3><p>A total of 167,984 predicted polypeptides from <i>R. communis</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>Carica papaya</i> were subjected to an all-versus-all BLASTP analysis using WU-BLASTP 2.0MP, with the default BLOSUM62 substitution matrix, no low-complexity sequence filter, and an E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>. The castor bean subset of the BLAST results was analyzed to extract 5,536 pairs of castor genes that are reciprocal best hits and reside on distinct sequence contigs.</p><p>Each of the 721 (of 25,828) castor scaffolds with at least five annotated protein-coding genes was examined for runs of five or more genes that are collinear and are reciprocal best hits of collinear genes in another castor bean scaffold. Images were generated from these results and inspected manually for the presence of regions that appear to be triplicated in the castor bean genome, on the basis of overlapping runs of collinear matching gene pairs. The regions thus determined were also cross-checked against dot plots showing the relative positions of the paralogous gene pairs.</p><p>To further analyze these putative triplications four sets of Jaccard<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Jaccard, P. The distribution of the flora in the alpine zone. New Phytol. 11, 37–50 (1912)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2262">74</a></sup> orthologous (protein) clusters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Crabtree, J., Angiuoli, S.V., Wortman, J.R. &amp; White, O.R. Sybil: methods and software for multiple genome comparison and visualization. Methods Mol. Biol. 408, 93–108 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2266">18</a></sup> were computed between castor and each of the four other genomes: Jaccard clusters were first defined within each genome by taking all BLASTP matches with E value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>, ≥80% identity and ≥70% sequence coverage and then forming clusters by transitively merging all pairs of proteins with Jaccard coefficient ≥0.6. In the second step, pairs of Jaccard clusters in distinct genomes were merged if each contained a protein with a best hit in the other cluster, taking into consideration only BLASTP matches with E value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup> and ≥70% sequence coverage (but imposing no other restriction on percent identity). For each triplication, Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.1674#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d27256e2274">75</a></sup> was used to display the three castor regions and any collinear cluster matches between genes in those regions and those in the respective target genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Accession codes.</h3><p>GenBank nuccore: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AASG02000000">AASG02000000</a>; and GenBank gene: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=XP_002509419.1">XP_002509419.1</a>–<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=XP_002540639.1">XP_002540639.1</a>. (The annotation data can also be freely accessed through the project's website (<a href="http://castorbean.jcvi.org/">http://castorbean.jcvi.org/</a>), which includes a genome browser and a BLAST server.)</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 4 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Optimization%20of%20biodiesel%20production%20from%20castor%20oil&amp;journal=Appl.%20Biochem.%20Biotechnol.&amp;volume=130&amp;pages=405-414&amp;publication_year=2006&amp;author=da%20Silva%20Nde%2CL&amp;author=Maciel%2CMR&amp;author=Batistella%2CCB&amp;author=Maciel%20Filho%2CR">

Q.Z., J.O. and M.S. conducted genome annotation. D.P. worked on the genome assembly. A.M.-B., K.M.J. and J.R. made DNA preparations, library constructions, and closure work. G.C., E.B.C. and M.G. performed manual annotations. C.M.F.-L. and J.R. conceived the project.
